[1] Chapter 1: Murder Advertisement
[2] 1
[3] From Monday to Saturday, Johnny Barton would cycle around the village of Chipping Clegg Horn once every morning between 7:30 and 8:30, shouting loudly as he delivered the morning newspapers ordered by each household from Mr. Totman, the stationer on High Street. For example, he delivered The Times and The Daily Mail to the Eastbrook Colonel's household; The Times and The Worker's Daily to Mrs. Sweetnam's; The Daily Telegraph and The New Yearbook to Miss Chinchcliff and Miss Mulgartoyd's residence; and The Telegraph, The Times, and The Daily Mail to Miss Blacklock's.
[4] Every Friday, he would deliver The North Bampton News and The Chipping Clegg Horn Gazette, which is locally known as The Gazette, to these subscribers - actually, every household in the village.
[5] So, on Friday mornings, the headlines of the daily newspapers would be filled with such news:
[6] International crisis! The United Nations meets today! Blonde typist killed, detective in pursuit of the killer! Three coal mines closed. Food poisoning at the seaside hotel, twenty-three people tragically dead.
[7] After a quick glance at the above content, the residents of Chipping Clegg Horn would eagerly turn to the local news in The Gazette, diving into the gossip and local affairs. After scanning through the communications column filled with daily disputes and grievances, the subscribers would usually turn to the personal ads section. There were mixed advertisements for buying and selling, ads for hiring housekeepers, countless ads about dogs, ads about poultry and gardening tools, and other miscellaneous items that greatly interested the residents of this small town.
[8] The Friday of October 29th was no different.
[9] 2
[10] Mrs. Sweetnam smoothed the small lock of pretty gray curls on her forehead as she unfolded The Times. As usual, she glanced briefly at the central column to see if there were any breaking news; then she moved on to the births, marriages, and obituaries section, especially the latter; after she finished, she put The Times aside and eagerly picked up The Chipping Clegg Horn Gazette.
[11] By the time her son Edmund entered the room, she was already engrossed in the personal ads section.
[12] "Good morning, dear," said Mrs. Sweetnam. "The Smethley family is selling their Daimler, a 1935 model - it's been a while, hasn't it?"
[13] Her son grumbled as he poured himself a cup of coffee, took two slices of smoked fish, and sat down at the table, opening the "Worker's Daily" and placing it on the toaster rack.
[14] "Puppy, I don't understand how people can still afford large dogs - it's hard to imagine... hm, Serena Lawrence is advertising for a cook again. I should tell her that advertising is a waste of time these days. She didn't leave an address, only an email address - that's a mistake. Servants always want to know where they will be working. They like good locations... false teeth - I don't understand why they are so popular. High-quality lighting... affordable. Sounds tacky... Here's a girl looking for 'an interesting job - willing to travel'. Who wouldn't want that?... Dachshund... I've never really liked German small hunting dogs - not because they are German, that page turned long ago - I just don't like them, nothing more... What, Mrs. Finch?"
[15] A woman with an old velvet beret on her head peeked in through the doorway, her expression somber.
[16] "Good morning, ma'am," said Mrs. Finch. "Can I clear the table?"
[17] "Not yet. We're not finished yet," answered Mrs. Sweetnam, adding in a conciliatory tone.
[18] Mrs. Finch glanced at Edmund and his newspaper, sniffed, and then left.
[19] "I'm just getting started," said Edmund.
[20] And his mother quickly followed up with: "I really don't want you reading this terrible newspaper, Edmund. Mrs. Finch doesn't like it at all."
[21] "I don't see how my opinions concern Mrs. Finch."
[22] "It doesn't really matter," continued Mrs. Sweetnam. "You're like a worker yourself. You don't do any work."
[23] "That's not true," Edmund protested indignantly. "I'm writing a book."
[24] "I mean real work," said Mrs. Sweetnam. "And Mrs. Finch is so important. If she dislikes us, and doesn't come to work, who would we find?"
[25] "We could advertise in the newspaper," Edmund grinned.
[26] "I've told you that wouldn't work. Oh, dear, this year, if a family doesn't have an old maid to cook and take care of the house, there's no hope."
[27] "Well, where's our old maid then? You never hired one when I was a child, and that was a mistake. What were you thinking?"
[28] "You had a wet nurse then, dear."
[29] "Lack of foresight," Edmund muttered.
[30] Mrs. Swetnam went back to her personal ads column.
[31] "For sale: Used electric lawn mower. Let me see... Oh, heavens, what a price!... Another Dachshund... 'Desperate Collar Circle, Seeking Correspondence.' What a stupid name... Cocker Spaniel... Do you remember our dear Susie, Edmund? It was so intuitive... For sale: Sherraton-style cabinet. Authentic heirloom. Contact: Lucas of Darcy Manor... That woman can spin a tale! Saying it's a Sherraton!"
[32] Mrs. Swetnam snorted and continued reading.
[33] "All misunderstandings, dear. Always loving you. Friday as usual. F" ... Sounds like a lovers' spat - or maybe a thief's code, what do you think? ... Here comes another Dachshund! I swear, it seems people are obsessed with breeding Dachshunds. I mean, there are other breeds out there. Your Uncle Simon used to have a Manchester Terrier - such a dainty little thing. I do like dogs that have legs... For sale: navy blue two-piece suit for a woman traveling abroad... No size, no price... Wedding announcement - no! A murder case. Oh, that's odd! Edmund, Edmund, listen to this..."
[34] A murder is scheduled to take place on Friday, October 29th at 6:30 PM at the Small Circle. Please be informed. Friends are requested to attend, and no further notice will be given.
[35] "That's extraordinary, Edmund!"
[36] "What's the matter?" Edmund looked up from the newspaper.
[37] "October 29th, Friday... Isn't that today?"
[38] "Let me see." The son took the newspaper from her hand.
[39] "What does it mean?" Mrs. Swetnam asked curiously.
[40] Edmund rubbed his nose skeptically.
[41] "I guess it's some kind of party. Murder mystery – that sort of thing."
[42] "Oh," Mrs. Sweetnam said doubtfully, "that seems too odd. Just an advertisement, not something I would expect from Letitia Blacklock. I always thought she was a sensible woman."
[43] "Maybe it's one of her lively young people."
[44] "The notice is too urgent. Today. Do you think we should go?"
[45] "It says, 'All friends are kindly requested to attend. No further notice will be given,'" her son pointed out.
[46] "Well, I think that's a rather tacky way to invite people," said Mrs. Sweetnam decisively.
[47] "Well, mom, you don't have to go."
[48] "That's right," agreed Mrs. Sweetnam.
[49] She fell silent for a moment.
[50] "Are you really going to eat that last piece of bread, Edmund?"
[51] "I think it's more important to properly absorb nutrients than to let that old hag clear the table," he replied.
[52] "Shh, dear, she'll hear you... Edmund, what's this game of murder about?"
[53] "I'm not entirely sure... they put papers on you or something... No, wait, I think they draw straws from a hat. Someone is the victim, someone is the detective – then they turn off all the lights, someone taps you on the shoulder, and you scream and pretend to be dead."
[54] "That sounds quite interesting," she said.
[55] "I'm afraid it's terribly dull. I won't be going."
[56] "Nonsense, Edmund," Mrs. Swetnam insisted, "I'm going, and you're coming with me. It's settled."
[57] 3
[58] "Archibald," Mrs. Eastbrook said to her husband, "listen to this."
[59] Colonel Eastbrook ignored her, as he was reading an article in The Times with disdain.
[60] "The problem with these people," he said, "is that they know nothing about the real situation in India! They know nothing!"
[61] "Yes, dear, you're right."
[62] "If they knew, they wouldn't write this drivel."
[63] "Yes, you're right, Archibald, you must listen to this."
[64] A murder is scheduled to take place on Friday, October 29th at 6:30 PM at Little Pond, please be aware. Friends are requested to attend, no further notice will be given.
[65] She paused triumphantly. Colonel Eastbrook looked at her fondly but showed little interest.
[66] "Murder mystery," he commented.
[67] "Hmm."
[68] "Let me tell you, it's just that, if organized properly, it can be quite interesting. But it needs a professional to plan it. Everyone draws a lot, and one person becomes the killer, with no one knowing who it is. When the lights go out, the killer picks someone to kill. The victim has to count to twenty before screaming. Then the person playing the detective takes over, questioning everyone about where they were and what they were doing when the murder happened, in order to find the real killer. Yes, it's quite an interesting game – if the detective, uh, has some understanding of police work."
[69] "Like you, Archie. You've solved many interesting cases in your jurisdiction."
[70] Colonel Eastbrook smiled, stroking his small beard with pride.
[71] "Yes, Laura," he replied, "I dare say I could give them a hand."
[72] He straightened his shoulders.
[73] "Miss Blacklock should have asked you to help her set it up."
[74] The colonel snorted.
[75] "Ah, well, she does have a young man staying there. Probably his idea. Her nephew or something. But the idea of advertising it is quite interesting."
[76] "We might not see it in the personal notices. I suppose it's an invitation, Archibald?"
[77] "This invitation is ridiculous. One thing I can tell you, they don't need me."
[78] "Oh, Archibald!" exclaimed Mrs. Eastbrook, with a touch of reproach.
[79] "The notice was too sudden. Besides, they know I'm busy."
[80] "But you're not busy, are you, dear?" Mrs. Eastbrook lowered her voice and spoke earnestly. "And I really think, Archibald, you should go – just to help poor Miss Blacklock. I'm sure she's looking forward to you going and putting things right. I mean, you're so familiar with police work and procedure. If you don't go and help, the whole thing will be a failure. Besides, we must have a sense of community spirit, mustn't we?"
[81] Mrs. Eastbrook tilted her head slightly, with her blue eyes wide open, wearing her golden wig.
[82] "If you say so, Laura," Colonel Eastbrook stroked his gray mustache, looking affectionately at his petite wife, who was at least thirty years younger than him.
[83] "Since you say so, Laura," he said.
[84] "I really think it's your duty, Arch," Mrs. Eastbrook replied solemnly.
[85] 4
[86] The Chipping Cleghorn Gazette was delivered to the Gravel Pit House, where the two charming cottages had been joined together to form a single residence for Miss Chinchy and Miss Muggleton.
[87] "Chinchy?" asked Miss Muggleton.
[88] "What is it, Muggleton?" asked Miss Chinchy.
[89] "Where are you?" asked Miss Muggleton.
[90] "In the hen house."
[91] "Oh."
[92] Miss Muggleton cautiously made her way across the long, wet grass to her friend. The latter was dressed in corduroy trousers and a military-style short jacket, busily stirring in a steaming pot filled with boiled potato skins and cabbage heads, adding a mixture of ingredients.
[93] She turned to her friend. Her hair was cut short, like a man's crew cut, and her face was weather-beaten. Miss Muggleton, on the other hand, was plump and affable, wearing a plaid skirt and a faded blue sweater. She was slightly out of breath, with her gray hair in a mess like a bird's nest.
[94] "It's in the Gazette," she gasped. "Listen carefully - what does this mean?"
[95] A murder will take place on Friday, October 29th at 6:30 PM at the Little Hall. Please be informed. Friends are requested to attend; no further notice will be given.
[96] She paused after reading, waiting for a response from the other party.
[97] "Stupid," said Miss Chickiff.
[98] "Yes, but what do you think it means?"
[99] "Well, at least there'll be drinks," replied Miss Chickiff.
[100] "Do you think this is an invitation?"
[101] "We'll see when we get there," Miss Quincey said. "I suppose it'll be some terrible sherry. You'd better move off the grass, Muggleton. You're still in your bedroom slippers and they're soaked."
[102] "Oh, dear!" Miss Muggleton regretfully looked at her feet. "How many eggs today?"
[103] "Seven. That damned hen's still brooding, I must put her in the coop."
[104] "It's rather ridiculous, don't you think?" Amy Muggleton picked up the newspaper again, with a slight longing in her voice.
[105] But her friend remained unmoved, her mind fixed on the stubborn fowl. No matter how mysterious and bizarre the advertisement in the newspaper, it could not affect her.
[106] She tramped heavily through the mud, and then rushed at a speckled hen with a comb. An angry cluck came from her.
[107] "It would be so much easier to have a duck," said Miss Quincey, "but they're so much more trouble..."
[108] 5
[109] "Ah, splendid!" said Mrs. Harmon to her husband, the Rev. Julian Harmon, sitting opposite her at the table. "Miss Blacklock is going to have a murder."
[110] "A murder?" he asked, rather surprised. "When?"
[111] "Today afternoon…not later than tonight. Six thirty, oh, dear, you'll have to prepare for the confirmation tonight. It's such a pity. You do like murders so much!"
[112] "I don't understand what you're talking about, roundabout."
[113] Mrs. Harmon's round face and shape were fitting for her baptismal name of Diana, which had long since been replaced by the nickname "roundabout." She passed the newspaper across the table.
[114] "There. It's between the classified ads for second-hand pianos and false teeth."
[115] "This ad is quite unusual."
[116] "Isn't it?" Roundie replied cheerfully, "You wouldn't think Miss Blacklock would be interested in murder or murder games, would you? I suppose it was the young Simmons siblings who persuaded her to place it - I thought Julia Simmons would find murder quite cruel. But it's there in black and white, and I really think, dear, you can't miss it. But I'll go, and I'll tell you all about it when I get back. Although I'll go anyway, because I really don't like playing games in the dark. It scares me, and I really wish I wouldn't be the first to die. If someone suddenly puts a hand on my shoulder and whispers, 'You're dead,' my heart would pound so hard, it might actually kill me! Do you think that's possible?"
[117] "No, Roundie, I think you'll live a long time, until you become an old lady - with me."
[118] "And die together on the same day, buried together. How nice."
[119] At the thought of this pleasant future, Roundie beamed with happiness.
[120] "You seem very happy, Roundie?" her husband asked with a smile.
[121] "Who wouldn't be happy with you, Susan, and Edward, who like me and don't think I'm silly... and with such a lovely big house to live in!"
[122] Reverend Julian Harmon looked around the spacious, empty dining room with a doubtful expression.
[123] "Some people would find it terrible to live in such a big, untidy, drafty place."
[124] "Oh, I like spacious houses. Fresh air can flow in. And even if you don't tidy up, things won't look messy."
[125] "Without any conveniences, and no central heating? That means you'll have a lot of work to do, Round."
[126] "Oh, Julian, no. I get up at 6:30, light the furnace, and then work like a steam engine for a while, and by 8:00, everything is done. And I do it well, right? Decorate the room with beeswax, polish, and big jars of autumn leaves. Running a big house is no more difficult than a small one. Sweeping and wiping the table are much faster because there is nothing to bump into behind, unlike a small house. Moreover, I like sleeping in a cold big room - I can lie down comfortably, only the tip of my nose can feel what it's like outside the quilt. Anyway, no matter how big the house is, the amount of peeled potatoes and washed dishes is the same. Besides, think about how much fun Edward and Susan have playing in the big room! They can spread out toy train tracks and tea sets for dolls all over the floor without having to clean up. And it's nice to have a few rooms for guests to stay in. Unlike Jimmy Smythe and Johnny Finch, who can only live with their in-laws. And you know, Julian, living with your in-laws is not good. You're very filial to your mother, but you won't be happy to really live with your parents after getting married, and I won't either. Then I'll always feel like a little girl. "
[127] Julian smiled at her.
[128] "You're still like a little girl, Round Round."
[129] Reverend Julian Harmon, at the age of sixty, was a fine specimen of nature's creation, as he looked twenty-five years younger than he actually was.
[130] "I know I'm silly."
[131] "You're not silly, Roundy, you're clever."
[132] "No, I'm not clever, I'm not at all smart, even though I try... I like it when you read to me, tell me history and other things. And in the evenings, reading Gibbon's works to me seems a bit foolish. Because some things in Gibbon's books make you want to fall asleep when it's cold outside and the fire is warm and cozy."
[133] Julian laughed.
[134] "But I do like listening to you read, Julian. Tell me that old parson's story about Ahasuerus again."
[135] "You can recite it, Roundy."
[136] "Tell me again, please."
[137] So her husband obliged and began telling the story.
[138] "This is an old pastor named Skrinker. One day, someone went to his church, and he was leaning on the pulpit, preaching to two old cleaning women. He wagged his finger at them and said, 'Ah-ha! I know what you're thinking. You're thinking that the first verse [4] of Ahasuerus the Great is Artaxerxes II. But he's not! He's Artaxerxes III.' He announced triumphantly."
[139] Julian always thought the story wasn't particularly funny, but it always made Roundy laugh.
[140] Her laughter rang out.
[141] "Poor old dear," she exclaimed. "I think someday you'll be just like him, Julian."
[142] Julian looked quite uncomfortable.
[143] "I know," he concurred humbly. "I do feel that sometimes I can't find a simple and appropriate way."
[144] "I'm not worried," Roundy said, standing up and stacking the breakfast dishes onto a large tray. "Mrs. Bartlett told me yesterday that Mr. Bartlett, who never used to go to church and was a local atheist, now goes every Sunday just to hear you preach."
[145] She imitated Mrs. Bartlett's affected tone, continuing:
[146] "'And one day, madam, Mr. Bartlett said to Mr. Timkins from Worsdale, we at Chipping Clegg-horn are the real cultural ones. Unlike Mr. Gross from Worsdale, who speaks to the congregation as if they were all uncultured children. True culture, Mr. Bartlett said, is our advantage. Our clergyman is a highly educated gentleman. From Oxford, not Milchester, and he imparts all the benefits of his education to us. He knows all about Romans, Greeks, Babylonians, Assyrians, even the cat of the parsonage, Mr. Bartlett said, is named after a king of Assyria!' So, that's your glory." Roundabout concluded her speech triumphantly, "Well, I must get back to work, or I'll never finish. Here, Tigris and Euphrates, have your herring bones."
[147] She opened the door, propped it open with her foot, and hurried off with the tray of cutlery, singing in a loud but slightly off-key voice:
[148] It's a fine day to kill time,
[149] Like a warm May day,
[150] With the town's police nowhere to be found.
[151] The sound of clattering dishes being placed in the sink drowned out the next line. But as Julien Harmon left the house, he heard the final triumphant and daring line of the song:
[152] Murder's on the scene today.
[153] * * *
[154] [1] A nickname for Wuthering Heights.
[155] [2] Confirmation: a Christian ceremony in which a person who has been baptized is confirmed as a member of the church. According to Christian doctrine, a child must be baptized and confirmed before they can become a full member of the church.
[156] Edward Gibbon (1737-1794), a British historian.
[157] A section of Scripture read during the morning prayer service in the Church of England.
[158] Chapter 2: Breakfast Mystery
[159] 1
[160] In the small courtyard, breakfast was also being served.
[161] Miss Blacklock was the mistress of the house, over sixty years old, sitting at the head of the table. She wore a country-style tweed suit and a short necklace made of large imitation pearls, which looked a bit out of place with her outfit. She was reading the section of the Daily Mail about the events on Necot Street. Julie Simmons was browsing the Telegraph in a lackluster manner, Patrick Simmons was examining the crossword puzzle in the Times, and Miss Dora Bunner was immersed in the local weekly newspaper.
[162] Miss Blacklock was chuckling to herself. Meanwhile, Patrick grumbled, "It's 'sticking' instead of 'adhesive' [1] – I got caught on that word."
[163] Suddenly, there was a loud "cluck" from Miss Bunner's side.
[164] "Lettie [2] – Lettie – did you see this? What does this mean?"
[165] "What's the matter, Dora?"
[166] "An unusual notice, clearly inviting everyone to the small park. But what does it mean?"
[167] "Let me see, dear Miss Bonner -"
[168] Miss Bonner handed the newspaper to Miss Blacklock, who reached out and took it, then pointed to the notice with her index finger.
[169] "Look here, Lettie."
[170] Miss Blacklock looked at it, raised her eyebrows, and then loudly read out the notice.
[171] "A murder will take place at Little Park on Friday, October 29th at 6:30 p.m. Please be informed. Friends are requested to attend, no further notice will be given."
[172] "Patrick, is this your doing?" she asked sharply, her gaze falling on the young man at the other end of the table, whose handsome face was well-known to all.
[173] Patrick Simmons immediately denied it.
[174] "No, it's not, Aunt Letty. How could you think that? Why should I know about it?"
[175] "Because you're the kind of person who would do something like that,” Miss Blacklock said acerbically, "to pull a prank or something."
[176] "Prank? Not a chance."
[177] "You, Julia?"
[178] "Of course not," Julia answered listlessly.
[179] "You think, Mrs. Hyde-Miller..." Miss Bonner trailed off, glancing at the empty seat at the table.
[180] "I don't think our Philippa would attempt such a whimsical thing," Patrick interjected. "She's a serious girl, really."
[181] "But what is the purpose?" Julia yawned, "What does it mean?"
[182] Miss Blacklock spoke slowly, "I suppose... it's some kind of silly trick."
[183] "But why?" Miss Bonner exclaimed, "What's the point? It looks like a silly joke, and very poor taste."
[184] Her slack cheeks trembled with anger, and her myopic eyes flashed with fury.
[185] Miss Blacklock smiled at her.
[186] "Don't worry about it, Bonnie," she said. "It's just some guy's idea of a joke, but I'd like to know who's behind it."
[187] "It says tonight," Miss Bunner pointed out, "Six thirty tonight. What do you think will happen?"
[188] "Death!" Patrick said grimly, "The Death of Delight."
[189] Miss Bunner gave a little shriek. "Shut up, Patrick," Miss Blacklock said.
[190] "I was only referring to the special cake that Micky makes," Patrick said apologetically. "You know we always call it the Death of Delight."
[191] Miss Blacklock smiled vaguely.
[192] Miss Bunford persisted. "But, Letty, do you really think—"
[193] Her friend interrupted her with a reassuring and comforting tone.
[194] "As for what will happen at six thirty," she announced dryly, "half the village will be here, all with their curiosity piqued. I'd better have some sherry on hand."
[195] 2
[196] "You're worried, aren't you, Lotte?"
[197] Miss Blacklock was startled into looking up from her place at the writing table, where she had been idly sketching small fish on blotting paper. Now she raised her head to look into her friend's anxious face.
[198] She didn't know what to say to Miss Bonner, for she knew that Miss Bonner could not bear any more anxiety or sorrow. Miss Blacklock fell silent, lost in thought.
[199] She and Miss Bonner had been at school together. Miss Bonner had been a golden-haired beauty, not very bright, but her looks and vivacious personality had made her popular. She must have - Miss Blacklock reflected - married a decent officer or a country lawyer. She had so many redeeming qualities: kindness, devotion, loyalty, but life had been hard on Miss Bonner, forcing her to fend for herself. Despite her best efforts, she had never quite managed to make ends meet.
[200] These two friends had not seen each other for some time. However, six months ago, Miss Blacklock had received a letter from Miss Bonner, disjointed in thought and full of sorrow. Miss Bonner's health was deteriorating, and she lived alone in a tiny cottage, subsisting on a pension. She tried to make ends meet by doing some needlework, but her fingers were stiff from rheumatism. In her letter, she talked about their school days - before life had forced them apart - but could her old friend offer her any help?
[201] Miss Blacklock had impulsively written back. Poor Miss Bunner, poor, pretty, stupid, and shallow Miss Bunner. She had rushed to Miss Bunner's aid, bringing her back to settle in the country, and concocting excuses to comfort her: "There are too many chores for me to handle alone, so I need someone to help me manage the household." However, before long - the doctor had warned her - she would often feel that bringing Miss Bunner was a mistake. Miss Bunner made a mess of everything, causing the eccentric foreign "helper" to become agitated; she would count the laundry wrong, misplace bills and letters, and sometimes irritate the capable Miss Blacklock to the point of anger and pain. However, poor and confused Miss Bunner was so loyal and eager to help, feeling so happy and proud to be able to do something for others - unfortunately, she was utterly unreliable.
[202] Miss Blacklock spoke sharply.
[203] "Don't do that, Miss Bunner. You know I told you -"
[204] "Oh," Miss Bunner said apologetically, "I know. I forgot, but - but you're worried, aren't you?"
[205] "Worried? No," she added sincerely, "at least, not very. Are you talking about the silly advertisement in The Times?"
[206] "Yes. Even if it's a joke, it seems - it seems cruel."
[207] "Cruel?"
[208] "Yes. I just feel that there's something a bit cruel about it. I mean - it's not a nice joke."
[209] Miss Blacklock looked at her friend, regarding the soft eyes, long and obstinate mouth, the slightly upturned nose. Poor Dora, so caught up in her own world, so muddled, so involved, so irritating. A lovable and over-sensitive old fool, but strangely intuitive.
[210] "I think you're right, Dora," Miss Blacklock said. "It's not a nice joke."
[211] "I don't like it at all," Miss Dora Bonner said in a tone of unusual firmness, "It frightens me."
[212] "Nonsense," Miss Blacklock retorted sharply.
[213] "It's dangerous, that's what I feel about it, just as if someone had put a bomb in a parcel and sent it to you."
[214] "Darling, it's just some silly fool trying to play a trick."
[215] "But it's not funny."
[216] Indeed it was... Miss Blacklock's expression gave away her thoughts, and Miss Bunner triumphantly exclaimed: "There, you think so yourself!”
[217] "But, Miss Bunner, my dear...”
[218] She broke off. A young woman, her bosom swelling with emotion, her figure wrapped in a tight knit sweater, her lower half clad in a dazzlingly bright skirt, her oily dark hair coiled atop her head, dark eyes glowing, burst in at the door.
[219] "Can I speak to you? Can I, may I not, please?” She fired off her questions like a machine gun.
[220] Miss Blacklock sighed.
[221] "Of course, Mickey, what's the matter?"
[222] Sometimes she thought that it would be easier to do all the household chores and cooking herself rather than dealing with this refugee "helper" who was constantly moody.
[223] "I'm going to tell you - is that the right order? I'm going to notify you, I'm leaving - I'm leaving right now!"
[224] "Why? Has someone disturbed you?"
[225] "Yes, I'm very upset," Mickey said dramatically, "I don't want to die! I've escaped from the continent of Europe. My family is dead - all killed - my mother, little brother, and dear little niece - all, all killed. But I escaped - I hid. I came to England. I work. I do things that I would never do in my own country - I -"
[226] "I understand all that, Miss Blacklock," said the woman firmly. These words were always on Mickey's lips. "But why do you want to leave now?"
[227] "Because they're coming to kill me!"
[228] "Who's coming to kill you?"
[229] "My enemies. Nazis! Maybe this time it's the Bolsheviks. They found me here, they're coming to kill me. I saw it – yes – in the newspaper!"
[230] "Oh, you mean in The News?"
[231] "Here, it's right here." Midge took the newspaper she had been hiding behind and showed it to her. "See—it says there's a murder, right here in the park. That's here, isn't it? At six thirty tonight. Ah! I don't want to wait to be killed—no, I don't!"
[232] "But why should this be about you? This is—we think it's a joke."
[233] "A joke? Murder isn't a joke, is it?"
[234] "No, of course not. But, my dear child, if someone wanted to kill you, they wouldn't advertise it in the newspaper, would they?"
[235] "You don't think they would?" Midge seemed to shiver. "You don't think, perhaps, they don't really intend to kill anybody? Maybe they're after you, Miss Blacklock."

[429] George Ridestad, the Chief Constable of Middlesex, was a man of few words, of medium height, with shrewd, piercing eyes under bushy eyebrows. He was accustomed to listening rather than talking, and then, with a voice devoid of emotion, issuing a concise command - and that command was always obeyed by his subordinates.
[430] He was currently listening to Inspector Delmont Corrado's report. Corrado had officially taken charge of the case at Little Wraps, and Ridestad had summoned him back last night. Ridestad had high regard for Corrado, considering him to be a man of brains and imagination. What he appreciated even more was Corrado's strict self-discipline and steady demeanor, always double-checking every fact and keeping an open mind until the case was closed.

[510] Colado seized on the word.

[520] From her, he couldn't get any more information. Rudi Scherz seemed ordinary. He hadn't seen him the night before last. He didn't know - not at all - he emphasized this - Rudi Scherz was a fraud.
[521] Perhaps, thought Corradoch, that was the truth.

[691] 2
[692] With Fletcher's guidance, Corradoch found Mickey in the kitchen. She was rolling out dough, and looked up at him suspiciously as he entered the room.

[765] Darcy's Manor must have seen its fair share of hardship in the years of war. [889] Detective Kolar had to spend a long time at Colonel Eastbrook's and his wife's. He had to patiently listen to a lengthy discourse on the psychology of the case.
[890] "Psychological methods – these are the only important things now," the colonel told him. "You have to understand the criminal. For someone with much more experience than me, the entire trap is obvious. Why did he place the advertisement? Psychological reasons. He wanted to promote himself – to get everyone's attention. He had been ignored, perhaps the hotel staff looked down on him because he was a foreigner, or maybe a girl had rejected him. Nowadays, what kind of idols are in movies – gangsters – tough guys? Great, so he decided to be a tough guy. Violent robbery. A mask? A revolver? But he still needs an audience – he must have an audience. So he arranges an audience. And then, the moment of climax, his character loses control – he's not just a thief, but a murderer. He fires – aimlessly –"
[891] Inspector Corrado interrupted at this point. "You said 'aimlessly,' Colonel Eastbrook. So you think he didn't aim at a specific target – say, Miss Blacklock?"

He suddenly realized that all this – these tricks he was playing – were real. He fired the revolver – possibly killed someone – and that pushed him over the edge. So, in a blind panic, he turned the revolver on himself."

[950] Coleridge placed all the interview records on the desk in front of the Chief, who had just finished reading a telegram from Swiss police.
[951] "He has a criminal record," said Ridestad, "Well – no surprise there."
[952] "Yes, sir."
[953] "Jewelry... yes... forged documents for entry... oh... checks... a real con artist."
[954] "Yes, sir – in the small things."
[955] "Indeed. Small evils can lead to great disasters."
[956] "I disagree, sir."
[957] The director looked up.
[958] "Are you worried, Corrado?"
[959] "Yes, sir."
[960] "What is it? This is a clear-cut case, isn't it? Let's see what these people you questioned have to say."
[961] He moved the report in front of him and quickly went through it.
[962] "Common sense - inconsistencies and contradictions. Different people's accounts of tense moments are bound to be different. But the overall picture seems clear."
[963] "I know, sir - but this picture doesn't sit right with me. If you understand what I mean - it doesn't feel right."
[964] "Well, let's stick to the facts. Rudi Scherz took the 5:20 bus from Medenham to Chipping Cleghorne at 6:00, with a ticket and two witnesses. He walked in the direction of the small field after getting off the bus. He didn't put up much of a fight - probably from the front door - and got into the house. He fired a revolver at the people inside, hitting Miss Blacklock lightly and then killing himself. Whether it was an accident or suicide, there's not enough evidence. I agree that the reason for doing it is not convincing. But that's not the question we should be answering. The coroner's verdict is: possibly suicide - possibly an accident. Whatever the outcome, it's the same for us. We can write up the case report now."
[965] "So, you mean we can always rely on Colonel Eastbrook's psychological theories?" Corradino said dejectedly.
[966] Ridestad laughed.
[967] "After all, Colonel Eastbrook may be experienced," he said. "I dislike how people nowadays always throw around psychological terms - but we can't rule out that factor either."
[968] "I still feel that the whole incident is not right, sir."
[969] "Is there any reason to believe that someone in Chipping Creagour lied to you?"
[970] Corradoc hesitated.
[971] "I think the foreign girl knows more than she's letting on. But that could be my prejudice."
[972] "Do you think she might have conspired with this man? Let him in? Encourage him to commit the crime?"
[973] "Something like that. I think she might have. But that would mean there's something valuable in the house, money or jewels or something. But it doesn't seem to be the case. Miss Blacklock is adamant there's nothing valuable, and so are the others. All this can only lead us to assume there's something valuable in the house, but no one else knows -"
[974] "It sounds like a plot from a bestseller."
[975] "I agree it sounds absurd, sir. Moreover, Miss Bonner is convinced that Shurz attempted to murder Miss Blacklock."
[976] "So, from what you've told me—from her testimony—Miss Bonner—"
[977] "Ah, I agree, Inspector," Corradoch quickly interjected, "she's an absolutely unreliable witness, easily influenced by suggestions. Anyone could put something in her head—but interestingly, this idea is entirely her own theory—no one has suggested anything to her. Everyone denies it. She's finally broken from the pack. What she says is based solely on her impressions."
[978] "Why would Rudy Sherlock kill Miss Blacklock?"
[979] "That's the problem, Inspector. I don't know. Miss Blacklock doesn't know—unless she's a better liar than I think she is. Nobody knows. So it's probably not true."
[980] He sighed.
[981] "Cheer up, Corradoch," the commissioner said, "I'll take you out, and we'll have lunch with Sir Henry at the Meddenham Royal Spa Hotel. We'll try their best dishes."
[982] "Thank you, sir," Corradoch said, somewhat surprised.
[983] "You see, we received a letter -" The commissioner suddenly cut off his words as Sir Henry Clithering entered the room, "Ah, you're here, Henry."
[984] "Good morning, Delmon," Sir Henry said, this time in a more casual tone.
[985] "I have something for you, Henry," the commissioner said.
[986] "What is it?"
[987] "A personal letter from an old maid. She lives at the Royal Spa Hotel. It's some information she thinks may be useful to us regarding the case at Chipping Cleghorn."
[988] "An old maid," said Sir Henry, smugly, "What did I tell you? They hear everything, see everything. But they don't say what they hear and see, do they? What's this special talent of hers?"
[989] Laidlaw looked at the letter.
[990] "Just like my grandmother wrote," he complained, "Full of sharp points. Like spiders in the inkwell, all underlined. Started off by saying she hopes it won't take up too much of our valuable time, but may be of some slight assistance, etc., etc. What's her name? Jane - what - Mopple - no, Mapple, Jane Mapple."
[991] "My God," said Sir Henry. "How convenient. George, that's exactly the special talent, the one and only, four-star old maid. The super-old maid. She managed to get to Meddingham instead of sitting quietly at home in St. Mary Mead, just in time to stir up a murder case. Another murder to advertise - just to amuse Miss Mapple."
[992] "Well, Henry," said Ridderdale, with a touch of mockery. "I'm glad to meet your perfect lady. Come on! Let's go to the Pavilion and meet this lady. Look, Colonel Crook looks suspicious."
[993] "No, sir," answered Crook politely. But he was wondering if his godfather wasn't a bit too fond of exaggeration at times.
[994] 2
[995] Miss Jane Mapple, even if not quite as close to Crook's imagination as he had thought, was not far off. She was far kinder and older than he had imagined. She looked truly weather-beaten. Her hair was white, her pink face was deeply wrinkled, her blue eyes were soft and innocent, and she was wrapped in a thick woolen sweater. She had a woolen lace shawl draped over her shoulders and was busy knitting a baby blanket.
[996] When she saw Sir Henry, she was so excited that she couldn't speak coherently, and when introduced to the Inspector and Inspector Corrigan, she was even more excited.
[997] "Really, Sir Henry, it's such an honor... Such an honor. It's been so long since I saw you... Yes, my rheumatism has been terrible lately. Of course, I couldn't afford to stay at this hotel, but their prices are insane. But Raymond—my nephew Raymond West, you may remember him—"
[998] "Everyone knows him."
[999] "Yes. This charming boy's books have always been successful—he's proud of never writing anything cheerful. This charming boy insists on paying for all my expenses. And his charming wife, the artist, has made a name for herself. Mainly with her dead flowers in pots and broken combs. I never dared tell her, but I still admire Blair Leithon and Alma Tadema. Oh, look, I'm rambling again. And the Police Commissioner himself—I never expected—I'm so afraid of taking up his time—"
[1000] "A real old fool," Inspector Corrigan grumbled to himself, feeling annoyed.
[1001] "Go to the manager's private office," Laidlaw said. "We can talk there."
[1002] So, Miss Marple took off her woolen shawl and gathered her spare knitting needles, and then she joined them in the comfortable lounge of Mr. Rolleston, grumbling all the way.
[1003] "Well, Miss Marple, let's hear what you have to say," the Inspector said.
[1004] Miss Marple introduced her point in a surprisingly succinct manner.
[1005] "It was a check," she said. "He forged the check."
[1006] "He?"
[1007] "The young man who works at the counter here, the one who's supposed to have directed that robbery and shot himself."
[1008] "You mean he forged a check?"
[1009] Miss Maple nodded.
[1010] "Yes. I brought it.” She pulled the check out of her bag and placed it on the table. "Here, it came with the rest of my things this morning from the bank. You see, it was seven pounds, and he changed it to seventeen. He added a line in front of the seven, and then added a ten after the seven [3], and cleverly blurred the whole thing with a little smudge of ink. It was very well done. I think he's a professional. What do you think?"
[1011] "He picked the wrong person this time," said Sir Henry.
[1012] Miss Marple nodded in agreement.
[1013] "Yes, he shouldn't have gone too far down the criminal path. It was a mistake for him to target me. Busy young married women or love-struck girls – they don't care about the numbers on the check and won't check their account balances. But an old lady who's used to being thrifty – that's the wrong target. Seventeen pounds is a number I wouldn't sign. Twenty pounds is a fixed monthly expense. As for my personal expenses, I usually exchange seven pounds in cash – it used to be five pounds, but everything has gone up now."
[1014] "Perhaps he reminded you of someone?" Sir Henry asked, with a sly look in his eyes.
[1015] Miss Marple smiled and shook her head.
[1016] "You're a cheeky one, Sir Henry. Yes, he did. Fred Taylor, the fishmonger. He always adds an extra shilling to the pounds column. With everyone eating so much fish now, the bill gets longer, and many people don't add up the numbers themselves. There's always an extra ten shillings in his pocket, which is enough for him to buy a few ties and take Jesse Spragg - the girl from the haberdashery - to the movies. That's what these young fellows want to do. Well, when I first came here, my bill was off. I pointed it out to the boy, and he was very sincere in his apology and looked very guilty. But at the time, I thought to myself, 'You have a very deceptive gaze, young man,' and I meant the kind of stare that fixes you with its gaze."
[1017] Colonel Corcoran suddenly felt a surge of admiration. "Just like the real Jimmie Kelly," he thought, recalling the notorious con artist he had recently helped capture.
[1018] "Rudy Schlesinger is a glutton," said Ridley. "We found he had a criminal record in Switzerland."
[1019] "I guess he couldn't stay there and then came here with a fake ID?" Miss Marple asked.
[1020] "That's right," Ridley answered.
[1021] "He often goes out with the red-haired waitress in the dining room," Miss Marple said. "Fortunately, I don't think she's interested. She just likes someone who's a bit 'different.' He buys her flowers and chocolates, and British boys don't usually do that. Did she tell you everything she knows?” She suddenly turned to Kolarov. "Or did she hold back?"
[1022] "I'm not entirely sure," Kolarov said cautiously.
[1023] "I think she's still holding something back," Miss Marple said. "She looks worried. This morning, she sent me salted fish instead of herring, and she forgot to bring the milk jug. She's usually an excellent waitress. Yes, she's worried, afraid she might have to testify. But I hope—" Her blue eyes looked straight and frank, with a purely female, Victorian admiration for the handsome and masculine Kolarov Inspector, "you can convince her to tell you everything she knows."
[1024] Kolarov Inspector blushed, and Sir Henry smiled to himself.
[1025] "This could be important," Miss Marple said. "He might have told her who he was."
[1026] Ridderdale stared at her in amazement.
[1027] "Who?" he asked.
[1028] "I didn't express myself clearly. What I meant was who made him do it."
[1029] "So you think someone else made him do it?"
[1030] Miss Marple looked astonished.
[1031] "Oh, it's only natural, isn't it? I mean... a handsome young man like that... he'd pick up a little here, a little there... alter a small check, perhaps steal a few trinkets left behind by someone, or even take a bit of money from the cash register... all little things, really, just to have some ready cash so he could dress well and take a girl out, and so on. But suddenly, he went crazy, took out a revolver, and held everyone in the room hostage, even firing at people. He could never have done that - not at any time! He's not that kind of person. It doesn't make sense."
[1032] Corcoran took a sharp breath. Lady Dedlock had said it. The vicar's wife had said it. And his own feelings were growing stronger. It doesn't make sense. Now the old maid was saying it, too, in her slow, definite manner.
[1033] "Perhaps you could tell us, Miss Marple," he said, suddenly becoming very forceful, "what happened?"
[1034] She turned to him in surprise.
[1035] "But how would I know what happened? There's a report, of course, but it's very brief. I can make some guesses, but I lack concrete evidence."
[1036] "George," said Sir Henry, "would it be out of order for Miss Maple to see the transcript of Coradine's conversation with the people in Chipping Cleghorn? Would that be a breach of protocol?"
[1037] "It might be," said Ridesdale, "but I got to where I am by not being overly concerned with rules. She can look. I'd like to hear her opinion."
[1038] Miss Maple felt very embarrassed.
[1039] "I'm afraid you've always been too ready to take Henry's word for it. Henry has always been kind. He overvalues any small observations I've made. In fact, I have no special talents—none at all—just a little knowledge of human nature. I find people too trusting of others. And I fear I always believe the worst. It's not a good quality, but it's often proved right by a series of events."
[1040] "Here you are," said Ridesdale, handing her a stack of typing paper. "It won't take you long. After all, these people are like you—you've seen many of them. You may find something we've missed. The case is about to be closed, and we'd like to hear what an amateur detective has to say. I can tell you, Coradine is not satisfied. He said it doesn't make sense, just like you."
[1041] Miss Maple read the report in silence. Finally, she put down the typing paper.
[1042] "Very interesting," she sighed. "Everyone has their own opinion. What they saw—or thought they saw. Everything is so complicated, almost all trivial details, and it's hard to see any important clues—like finding a needle in a haystack."
[1043] Corrigan was disappointed. For a moment, he had thought that Henry's recommendation of the famous Miss Maple might be right. She might hit on something—old people's feelings are often very sharp. For instance, he couldn't hide anything from his Aunt Emma. Later, she told him that whenever he was about to lie, his nose would twitch.
[1044] However, the famous Miss Maple could only come up with some silly generalizations. He was annoyed with her, so he said rather rudely, "The point is, the facts are indisputable. No matter how contradictory the details these people provide, they saw the same thing. They saw a masked man with a revolver and a flashlight, holding them up. Whether they thought he said 'put up your hands' or 'money or your life' or some other jargon related to their idea of a hold-up, they saw him."
[1045] "But, of course," said Miss Maple mildly, "they couldn't possibly—in fact—they couldn't possibly have seen anything…"
[1046] "If I understand correctly," Miss Marple's cheeks flushed, her eyes sparkled with childlike delight, "there was no light in the hallway outside - and none on the stairs?"
[1047] "Yes," Coricardo said.
[1048] "So, if there was a man standing at the door, with a powerful flashlight in his hand, shining it into the room, the people inside would see only the flashlight beam, right?"
[1049] "Yes, only the flashlight beam. I tried it."
[1050] "So, that's it," Miss Marple said, her face flushed with pleasure, her eyes sparkling with childlike delight. "There was no light in the hallway outside - and none on the stairs?"
[1051] "So, when someone says they saw a masked man, they are actually recalling what they saw later when the lights came on, even though they don't realize it. It all fits together, doesn't it? If we assume that Rudi Scherz is - I think the correct term would be 'a scapegoat'?"
[1052] Ridesdale's gaze upon Miss Marple was so surprised that her face reddened even more.
[1053] "I may have used the wrong word," she murmured, "I'm not very fluent in American English - the terms change so quickly. I learned the word 'scapegoat' from a story by Dashiell Hammett. I heard of him from my nephew Raymond, who is one of the 'hardboiled' literary trio. If I'm not mistaken, a 'scapegoat' refers to someone who takes the blame for others. In my opinion, Rudi Scherz seems to fit the bill. He's actually quite foolish, yet greedy and gullible."
[1054] Ridesdale suppressed a smile. "You're suggesting that someone persuaded him to shoot wildly at a room full of people with a gun? That's too far-fetched."
[1055] "I believe they told him it was a joke," Miss Marple said. "Of course, he was paid for it. That is, he was hired to place an advertisement in the newspaper, to investigate the mansion, and then to arrive there on the night of the incident, wearing a mask and a cloak, pushing open the door, and waving a flashlight while shouting 'Hands up!'"
[1056] "And then shoot and kill someone?"
[1057] "No, no," Miss Marple said. "He didn't have a revolver. "
[1058] "But everyone says –" Laidlaw began to speak but stopped.
[1059] "Quite right," Miss Marple said. "Even if he had a gun, no one would have seen it. And I believe he didn't. I think that after he shouted 'Hands up', someone quietly came up behind him, put the gun over his shoulder, and fired those two shots. This scared him so much that he suddenly turned around, and at that moment, the person behind him fired and then threw the gun next to him…"
[1060] The three men looked at her. Sir Henry spoke softly. "This theory could hold water."
[1061] "But who is this mysterious X who suddenly appeared in the dark?" the Commissioner asked.
[1062] Miss Marple cleared her throat.
[1063] "You'll have to find out from Miss Blacklock who wanted her dead."

[1065] "So you think it was deliberate?" Ridlesdale asked.

Whoever made the arrangements with Ruddy Shurlock must have worked very hard to keep him quiet; but if he did talk to anyone, it would probably be that girl, Mona Harris. He might - just possibly - have left some clues about who proposed the whole plan."
[1067] "I'll go and see her," said Colonel Draco, getting to his feet.

[1069] "Safer?... Yes, I see."
[1070] He left the room.
[1071] The superintendent remained skeptical but diplomatic: "Well, Miss Marple, you have indeed provided us with some food for thought."

[1073] "I'm sorry, really," said Mona Harris. "You're such a nice man, not getting angry. But you see, my mother is the type to get all worked up. I do look like...what was it?...a 'premeditated accomplice.' I mean, if I say I think it was just a joke, you wouldn't believe me."
[1074] Inspector Corrado repeated the assurances he had given to Mona Harris to ease her concerns.
[1075] "I'll tell you everything. But if possible, please don't involve me, okay? It's all because of Rudi Scherz and me going out. We had plans to go to the movies, and then he said he couldn't come, so I got a bit cold towards him because it was his idea, and I don't like standing next to a foreigner. He said it wasn't his fault, and I said he could make up any excuse he wanted, and then he said he was going to pull a prank that night, and he wouldn't have to pay for it, and asked if I wanted a watch. So I asked him what prank he was talking about, and he said not to tell anyone, and there was going to be a gathering somewhere, and he was going to pull off a robbery. He showed me the ad he had placed, and I couldn't help but laugh. He seemed to think it was childish, but British people are like that – they never grow up – of course, I asked him what he meant by us, and we argued, but then made up. Later, I saw the news in the paper, and I realized it wasn't a joke, and that Rudi Scherz had shot someone and then shot himself. At the time, my feelings, sir, only you can understand, can't you? – Oh, I don't know what to do. I thought if I said I knew about it beforehand, it would make me look like I was involved in the whole thing. But when he talked about it, it really seemed like a joke. I swear he meant it that way. I didn't even know he had a revolver. He never said he was going to bring a gun."
[1076] Chief Inspector Corduk comforted her and then asked the most important question.

[1082] He didn't smile for long, Colodoc thought.

[1115] 1
[1116] "I'm sorry to bother you again, Miss Blacklock—"
[1117] "Ah, it's no problem. Considering the investigation was halted for a week, I suppose you want more evidence?"
[1118] Inspector Corridock nodded.
[1119] "Firstly, Miss Blacklock, Rudi Scherz is not the son of the innkeeper of the Alpine Lodge in Monte Largo. His criminal activities began when he was a kitchen helper at a hospital in Bern. Many of the patients there lost small pieces of jewelry. He worked under another name at a winter sports resort, where he was adept at copying two bills in the restaurant, one without the item in question but appearing on the other. The difference, of course, went into his pocket. After that, he worked at a department store in Zurich. During his time there, the store suffered losses due to stolen goods that exceeded the average level. It seems likely that not all of the thefts were the fault of the customers."
[1120] "So, he's been known to take small things without permission, then?" Miss Blacklock said dryly. "I suppose I was right to say I hadn't seen him before?"
[1121] "You're quite right — undoubtedly, you were pointed out to him at the Royal Spa Hotel, so he pretended to recognize you. The Swiss police forced him to leave the country, so he came here with a beautifully forged set of documents and found work at the Royal Spa Hotel."
[1122] "Quite a lucrative hunting ground," Miss Blacklock commented dryly. "The expenses are extremely high, and only the very wealthy stay there. I imagine some of them don't care about the bills."
[1123] "Yes," Inspector Corrado said. "There's a lot of potential for a big haul there."
[1124] Miss Blacklock frowned.

[1142] He used a metaphorical expression, although the thought had occurred to him that if Miss Maple's reasoning was correct, the literal meaning of his words would also hold true. In any case, this did not impress Miss Blacklock, who still looked suspicious.

[1299] The garden of the clergyman is particularly pleasant. A sudden warm flow of autumn descended upon England. [1300] He sat in the recliner, which was given to him by the energetic and round woman named Round, who was going to attend a gathering of mothers. [1301] It seemed like a familiar dream, so peaceful and comfortable, but a dangerous undercurrent was constantly growing, and comfort eventually turned into terror...

[1308] "Helpful?" asked the inspector, bewildered.
[1309] "Helpful in finding out whether people are really as they say they are," said Miss Marple.
[1310] She continued, "Because it's this very thing that worries you, isn't it? Since the war began, the world has changed in a particular way. For example, Chipping Cleghorn is very much like the place where I live, St. Mary Mead. Fifteen years ago, everyone knew everyone else's business. The families of the Bannetts, the Hartneills, the Price-Reedleys, the Weatherbys… their parents, grandparents, aunts, and uncles had lived there for generations. If a stranger came to live there, they usually had an introduction, or they had served together in the same regiment or on the same ship. If they were a complete stranger, goodness, everyone would want to know all about them before they felt satisfied."
[1311] She paused and nodded slowly.
[1312] "But it's not like that now. Every village is full of people who have no local connections and just settle down. The big houses are sold, the little cottages are bought and sold, and people just turn up – with no proof of who they are except what they say. You see, they come from all over the world – India, Hong Kong, China; people who were born in France, living in cheap houses in Italy and on strange islands; people who have enough money to retire. But they don't know each other. You can have a house full of things from Benares, and talk about "Tiffany" and "Jota Hakizuri" [1] – and have paintings from Taormina on the wall, but talk about British churches and libraries – just like Miss Inchcliff and Miss Muggleton. You might come from France, or have spent half your life in the East. Everyone accepts newcomers without a second thought. No one expects a letter of introduction from a friend saying that someone is a good person, someone from childhood… and so on."
[1313] And this, thought Cuddlecome, was the source of his worry. He couldn't understand. People were just faces and personalities, identified by ration books and identity cards… but with no photos or fingerprints. Anyone could get a suitable identity card – the ties that once bound the English countryside together were gone, and this was part of the reason. In towns, no one knows their neighbors. In the countryside, it's the same, but sometimes you feel like you do. And because of that faulty door, Cuddlecome knew that there was a neighbor in Lady Blakelock's drawing room who was far from the friendly exterior she showed…

[1315]

He knew that some people were using borrowed identity cards to travel around – mostly from those who had died unexpectedly in the city. There are organized groups that buy identity cards and forged ration cards and identity cards to defraud, and there have been over a hundred cases. You can check, but it takes time, and he lacked time because Mrs. Randall-Godley's days were numbered.

[1332] Chapter 11: Tea Time Chatter
[1333] If Letitia Blacklock seemed a little absent-minded when Mrs. Harmon brought her guests over for tea, it was almost impossible for the other guest - Miss Marple - to notice, as they were meeting for the first time.
[1334] This elderly lady's gossipy manner was charming. She almost immediately showed herself to be the kind of elderly woman who is constantly on the lookout for thieves.
[1335] "My dear," she assured the hostess, "they could come in anywhere. They're everywhere now, even with all the new American methods. I still believe in the old-fashioned devices: door hooks and a pair of bright eyes. They can pick locks and slip open door latches, but a copper hook and a pair of eyes can thwart them. Have you tried it?"
[1336] "We're not very good with door latches and copper hooks," Miss Blacklock replied cheerfully, "and there's not much to steal in the house anyway."
[1337] "Chain the front door," Miss Marple suggested, "and have the maid open it just a crack to see who's there before they can rush in."
[1338] "I think Mimi, our Central European, would like that idea."
[1339] "The robbery must have been absolutely terrifying for you," Miss Marple said, "and roundy has been telling me all about it."
[1340] "I was paralyzed with fear," roundy said.
[1341] "It was a dreadful experience," Miss Blacklock admitted.
[1342] "He tripped and shot himself, it seems, which was probably divine intervention. Thieves are so ruthless nowadays. How did he get in?"
[1343] "Oh, I'm afraid we don't lock the door very often."
[1344] "Oh, Letty," Miss Bonner exclaimed, "I forgot to tell you, the Inspector was very strange this morning. He insisted on opening the second door—you know, the one that wouldn't open—and he was looking for a key to unlock it, and he said it had been oiled, but I don't understand why, because—"
[1345] By the time she saw Miss Blacklock's gesture to stop talking, it was too late, so she stopped short, but her mouth was still open.
[1346] "Oh, Letty, I'm so—I mean, oh, I'm terribly sorry, Letty—oh, dear, I'm such a fool."
[1347] "It's all right," Miss Blacklock said, but she was annoyed. "I just think Chief Inspector Corridick doesn't want anyone to talk about it. I didn't know you were there, Mr. Harmon, when he was doing his experiment. Can you understand that, Mrs. Harmon?"
[1348] "Ah, yes," said Round, "we won't tell a soul, will we, Aunt Jane? But I'm wondering why—"
[1349] She fell into a reverie. Miss Bonner sat fidgeting, looking miserable, and finally couldn't resist blurting out, "I always say the wrong thing, oh, dear, Letty, I only make things worse for you."
[1350] Miss Blacklock quickly said, "You're my greatest comfort, Mr. Harmon. And, after all, there aren't many secrets in a small place like Chipping Cleghorn."
[1351] "Indeed, there isn't," said Miss Marple. "You know, the way news gets about here is most extraordinary. The servants, of course, are one thing, but it's not only that, because there aren't so many servants nowadays. And then there are the women who come in to do the housework, and they're probably worse, because they go about and spread it all over the place."
[1352] "Ah!" said roundly. "I see now! Of course, if that door could be opened, someone could slip out in the dark and rob - only it couldn't be - because it was the man from the Royal Baths who did it. Or was it?... No, I still don't understand..."
[1353] "So it all happened in this room?" asked Miss Marple, and then added apologetically, "I'm afraid you'll think me incurably curious, Miss Blacklock - but it's so exciting - like reading a story in the paper - I just want to hear it all through, from beginning to end, if you understand what I mean -"
[1354] At once, she heard roundly and Miss Bunner talking in a confused and rambling way, with Miss Blacklock occasionally correcting them.
[1355] In the meantime, Patrick came in, kindly participating in the recapitulation - and even playing the part of Rudi Schlesinger.
[1356] "Aunt Letty is there—in the corner of the arch—stand there, Aunt Letty."
[1357] Miss Blacklock obeyed, and they pointed out the bullet holes to Miss Marple.
[1358] "How remarkable—what a narrow escape!" she breathed.
[1359] "I was just going to give the guests their cigarettes," said Miss Blacklock, indicating the large silver cigarette box on the table.
[1360] "People are so careless with cigarettes," said Miss Bonner disapprovingly. "No one appreciates good furniture nowadays. Someone put a cigarette on this lovely table, look at it, how badly it's been burned. It's disgraceful."
[1361] Miss Blacklock sighed.
[1362] "Sometimes, I think you care too much about other people's things."
[1363] Miss Bunner loved her friend's possessions so fiercely that it seemed as if she were the true owner herself. Round was always fond of this very charming trait in Dora, and showed no signs of jealousy.
[1364] "It's a lovely table," Miss Marple said politely. "And that ceramic lamp is so pretty."
[1365] Miss Bunner, the recipient of the compliment, seemed to own the lamp herself, rather than Miss Blacklock.
[1366] "Very pretty, isn't it? Dresden. There's another pair, I think, in the other room."
[1367] "You know where everything is in the house, Dora - or you think you do," said Miss Blacklock in a friendly tone. "You care for my things more than I do."
[1368] Miss Bonner blushed.
[1369] "I do like nice things," she said, half defensively, half wistfully.
[1370] "I must admit," said Miss Maple, "that I have a few precious things of my own - things that bring back so many memories, you know. Like photographs. People don't take many photographs these days. I like to keep my nieces and nephews' baby and childhood pictures - and so on."
[1371] "You have a terrible picture of me when I was three," said Round Round, "holding a poodle and squinting."
[1372] "I believe your aunt has quite a collection of your pictures, Patrick," said Miss Marple, turning to Patrick.
[1373] "Oh, we're just distant relatives," said Patrick.
[1374] "I believe Eleanor sent me a picture of you as a baby, Patrick," said Miss Blacklock. "But I'm afraid I didn't keep it. I've forgotten how many children she had, and what their names were, until she wrote to say you were coming here. I was so surprised."
[1375] "Another sign of the times," said Miss Marple. "Nowadays people don't often recognize their young relatives. In the old days, when the whole family used to get together, it was unheard of."
[1376] "I met Patrick and Julia's mother at a wedding thirty years ago," Miss Blacklock said. "She was a very pretty girl."
[1377] "So she had such handsome and beautiful children," Patrick said with a grin.
[1378] "You have a beautiful photo album," Julia said. "Do you remember, Aunt Letty, we looked at it that day? Those hats!"
[1379] "And how we thought we were so fashionable," Miss Blacklock sighed.
[1380] "Don't worry, Aunt Letty," Patrick said. "Thirty years later, Julia will accidentally see a snapshot of herself and think the person in the photo isn't her!"
[1381] "Did you bring up that topic on purpose?" Round asked as they walked home with Miss Marple. "I mean, about the photos."
[1382] "Oh, dear, it's interesting to know that Miss Blacklock hasn't seen her two young relatives before," she replied. "I'm sure Inspector Corcoran would be interested to hear that."

It's a habit, really. Yes, I suddenly believed this, after reading Bernhard Jones' biography."

[1452] Constable Fletcher personally oversaw the security of the small estate.
[1453] Today was supposed to be Mickey's day off. She always took the eleven o'clock bus to Medingham. After discussing it with Miss Blacklock, Constable Fletcher took over as the housekeeper. Miss Blacklock and Dora Bunner went to the village. Constable Fletcher quickly got to work. The door was oiled to be ready for use. Whichever of them did it, the purpose was to leave the drawing room unnoticed once the lights went out. This ruled out Mickey, as she had no need to use that door.
[1454] Who else was left? Constable Fletcher thought, and he could rule out the neighbors. He couldn't see how they could have found the opportunity to oil the door and prepare it.
[1455] That left only Patrick and Julia Simmons, Philipa Hyde, and possibly Dora Bunner. The young Simmons siblings were in Milchester, Philipa Hyde was at work, and Constable Fletcher could search any secret in the house. However, it was disappointing that there was nothing suspicious about the house. Although Constable Fletcher was an expert in the electrical system, he couldn't find any signs that would cause the light bulb to burn out. He quickly checked all the bedrooms and found everything to be normal, which was frustrating. Philipa Hyde's room had some photos of a boy with serious eyes. Another was taken earlier; there was also a stack of letters from schoolchildren and a couple of theater programs. Julia's room had a drawer full of snapshots from the south of France. There were a few photos of the beach, and another of a villa nestled in a bed of ferns. Patrick's room had some mementos from his time in the navy. Dora Bunner's room had few personal items and seemed entirely normal.
[1456]However, Inspector Fletcher thought, someone in this house must have oiled that door.
[1457]Just then, a sound downstairs interrupted his thoughts. He rushed to the top floor and looked down.
[1458]Mrs. Sweetnam was walking through the hallway, carrying a basket. She glanced into the living room and then walked through the hallway into the dining room. When she came out, the basket was gone.
[1459]Inspector Fletcher made a slight noise, and a floorboard creaked under his feet, causing her to turn. She called up, "Is that you, Miss Blacklock?"
[1460] "No, Mrs. Sweetnam, it's me," Inspector Fletcher replied.
[1461] Mrs. Sweetnam gave a little shriek.
[1462] "Oh! You startled me. I thought it was another thief."
[1463] Fletcher descended the stairs.
[1464] "This house seems to have poor security," he said, "can anyone come and go like you?"
[1465] "I just bought some fruit," Mrs. Sweetnam explained. "Miss Blacklock wanted to make quince jelly, but she didn't have a quince tree. I left some for her in the dining room."
[1466] After that, she smiled.
[1467] "Ah, I see, you're asking me how I got in? Well, I came through the side door. We're always coming and going in each other's houses, Inspector. It's never dark enough to think of locking the door. I mean, what would happen if you brought something over and couldn't get in? That would be awkward, wouldn't it? Nowadays, it's different. You ring the bell, and the servant comes to the door."
[1468] Mrs. Swetnam sighed. "I remember in India," she said mournfully, "we had eighteen servants – eighteen, mind you, not counting the nurse. It was only natural. At home, before I was married, we always had three servants – although Mum always felt that not having a cook was a sign of poverty. I must say, life has become very odd, Inspector, although I know I shouldn't complain. The worst part is, so many coal miners keep catching parrot fever (or parrot disease) that they have to leave the mines to become gardeners, but they can't tell the difference between spinach and weeds."
[1469] As she approached the door, she added, "I won't take up any more of your time. I'm sure you're very busy and won't have any more problems, right?"
[1470] "Why do you say that something will happen, Mrs. Swetnam?"
[1471] "I was just wondering, seeing as you're here. I thought it might be the Black Hand. Will you tell Miss Blacklock about the quince?"
[1472] Mrs. Sweetnam left. Fletcher felt as if he had been struck a blow. He had always thought that the oil on the door was from someone inside the house, but now he realized he was wrong. Anyone outside could have waited until Miss Midge left, until Miss Letitia Blacklock and Miss Dora Bunner went out, and then come in. It was too easy. This meant that he couldn't rule out anyone who had been in the room that night.

[1539] Chapter 13: Morning in the Village (Continued)
[1540] Miss Marple walked out of the vicarage and along the alleyway leading to the main street.
[1541]She walked briskly with the sturdy walking stick of Mr. Julian Harmon, the vicar.
[1542]She passed by the Red Cow store and the butcher's shop, pausing for a moment in front of Mr. Elliott's antique shop. The shop was cleverly situated next to the "Bluebird" tea and coffee house, so that when the wealthy patrons stopped by for a cup of good tea and a slice of the so-called "homemade cake," they might be tempted by Mr. Elliott's tastefully decorated window display.
[1543]In the circular protruding antique window display, Mr. Elliott showcased items to suit all tastes. Two Watford-made glass wine glasses sat perfectly on a cold wine holder. A desk made of various shapes of walnut wood was unmistakably genuine. A table in the window displayed a variety of cheap door handles and oddities, including a few Dresden-patterned ceramic pieces, two strands of ugly-looking beaded necklaces, a mug with the inscription "Tribute to Trumbull," and a few pieces of Victorian-style silverware.
[1544]Miss Marple was intently gazing at the items in the window display. Mr. Elliott, like an old, fat spider, peered out from his web, calculating whether he could possibly catch this newly arrived "fly."
[1545]But as he decided that the "Tribute to Trumbull" gift was too expensive for the lady living in the vicarage (Mr. Elliott, of course, knew exactly who she was), Miss Marple, through the corner of her eye, saw Miss Dora Bunner enter the "Bluebird" coffee house. She decided then and there that she must have a cup of morning coffee to fend off the chill.
[1546] Several ladies were already inside the coffee shop, taking a break from their morning shopping. Miss Marple looked around the dimly lit interior of the "Bluebird" and pretended to be browsing casually. Suddenly, Miss Bunner's voice called out to her:
[1547] "Ah, good morning, Miss Marple. Come and sit here with me. I'm all alone."
[1548] "Thank you."
[1549] Miss Marple gratefully sat down in the hard, blue-painted wooden armchair provided by the "Bluebird."

[1551] "Ah, I understand. I had sciatica for a year – it was quite painful most of the time."

[1676] Chapter 14: Looking Back
[1677] After a night on the train, Inspector Cordiner alighted at a small station in the Scottish Highlands.
[1678] For a moment, he was surprised that the wealthy Mrs. Goddard — a disabled woman — had chosen to live in the remote Scottish home instead of the fashionable square in London, the Hampshire estate, or the villa in the south of France. She must have cut herself off from many friends and entertainment. This must be a lonely life — or was she so sick that she couldn't notice or care about her surroundings anymore?
[1679] A car waited to pick him up, an old-fashioned, spacious Daimler driven by an elderly driver. It was a sunny morning, and during the twenty-mile drive, the inspector enjoyed himself despite being surprised by this choice for seclusion. A casual remark opened the driver's floodgates, giving him a rough understanding of the reason behind the decision.
[1680] "It's her home before she got married. Oh, she's the last one of the family. She was happier here with Mr. Goddard than anywhere else, even though he couldn't often get away from London. But whenever he came, they were like two children. Happy."
[1681] As the ancient mansion's gray walls came into view, Inspector Corrado felt as though time had reversed. An elderly male butler greeted him, and after freshening up, the inspector was led to a room with a roaring fireplace, where he had breakfast.
[1682] After breakfast, a middle-aged nurse dressed in a nurse's uniform entered the room, introducing herself as McLean Nurse.
[1683] "My patient is ready to see you, Inspector Corrado. She's been looking forward to meeting you.”
[1684] "I'll do my best not to upset her," Corrado promised.
[1685] "I should warn you about what to expect. You'll find Mrs. Goddard to be perfectly normal. She'll talk and enjoy talking, and then - suddenly - her energy will collapse. At that point, please leave immediately and call me. You'll see that she's mostly kept alive by morphine. Most of the time, she's in a half-conscious state. I've given her a shot of stimulant to stay awake for your visit. As the stimulant wears off, she'll return to her semi-comatose state."
[1686] "I understand, Nurse MacLane. Would it be appropriate for me to ask about Mrs. Goddard's exact health condition?"
[1687] "Well, Mr. Corrado, she is a dying woman. Her life expectancy is only a few weeks. If you find it strange that she has not passed away years ago, you may be right, but that is the truth. What has kept Mrs. Goddard alive is her intense desire and love for life. For someone who has suffered for years and has not left her home in fifteen years, it may seem unusual, but it is true. Mrs. Goddard has never been a robust woman, yet her will to survive has been remarkable. She is still a very charming woman, you will find."
[1688] Corrado was led into a large bedroom with a fire burning, where an elderly woman lay on a bed with a canopy. Although she was only a few years older than Letitia Blacklock, her frail body made her look much older.
[1689] Her hair was neatly combed, and a light blue woolen wrap covered her neck and shoulders. The lines on her face expressed pain, but there was also sweetness. It was strange that her faded blue eyes sparkled with what Corrado could only describe as a mischievous gaze.
[1690] "Well, this is quite interesting," she said. "I don't often have police visits. I hear that Letitia Blacklock was not seriously injured in that attack? How is my dear Blanche?"
[1691] "She is fine, Mrs. Goddard. She sends her regards."
[1692] "I haven't seen her in a long time... Many years have passed since she returned to England, and I invited her to stay here, but she said that after so much time, meeting old acquaintances would be painful. Perhaps she was right... Blythe is a very wise woman. About a year ago, an old school friend of mine came to visit me, but, hmm!"
[1693] She smiled slightly. "We looked at each other with disgust. After asking 'Do you remember?' there was nothing more to say. It was quite awkward."
[1694] Mr. Coleridge willingly let her ramble on, hoping to gain insight into the supposed family atmosphere between the Goddards and Blacklock.
[1695] "I suppose," Beryl shrewdly asked, "you want to know about the money? Randall left a will stating that the money should go to Blythe upon my death. Of course, Randall never thought I would outlive him. He was a big, strong man who never got sick; I was always complaining about aches and pains. Doctors came every few days, looking at my condition with a frown."
[1696] "I don't think 'complaining' is the right word, Mrs. Goddard."
[1697] The old lady chuckled.
[1698] "I didn't mean complaining in the sense of whining. I never felt too sorry for myself. But being so weak, everyone naturally thought I would go first. But it wasn't that way. No, not at all."
[1699] "Exactly, why did your husband do that with his money?"
[1700] "You mean why did he leave it to Blair? Not for the reasons you might imagine."
[1701] Kroll looked at the frail old lady on the big bed with interest. He realized that Bella Goddard had always enjoyed and still enjoyed being a woman. She blinked at him.
[1702] "And I've always thought," she said, "that being a man must be terribly boring."
[1703] Then she said thoughtfully, "I think Randall mainly saw Blanche as a brother. He relied on her judgment, and her judgment was always so good. You know, she's helped him out of difficulties more than once."
[1704] "She told me she saved him once with her money?"
[1705] "Yes, but I mean more than that. After all these years, I can tell the truth. Randall can't tell right from wrong, he's slow to feel, this poor dear doesn't know what cunning or deceit is. Blanche kept him from going astray. Letitia Blacklock has a quality that is absolutely upright. She would never do anything dishonest. Her character is excellent, you know. I've always admired her. They had a hard life when they were girls. Their father was a country doctor - slow-witted and obstinate - a tyrant in the house. Letitia ran away to England and trained to be a certified accountant. Her sister is somewhat disabled, probably deformed somewhere, so she never sees anyone and stays at home. So, when the old man died, Letitia gave up everything and went home to take care of her sister. Randall was angry with her - but it didn't matter. [1706] "How long before your husband died?"
[1707] "Two or three years, I think. Randall left his will before he died, and it hasn't been changed. He said to me, 'We have no children.' (Our little boy, you know, died when he was two.) [1708] "You see," Bella continued, "Randall enjoyed making money - not just having it - but the excitement, the risks, and the thrill. Blanche liked all that too. She has the same adventurous spirit and the same decisiveness. [1709] Kradok was surprised: a woman who had suffered from chronic illness her whole life, her only child had died, and her husband had died. She lived a lonely widowhood and had been a hopeless invalid for years. Yet she had genuine compassion for others and scorned pain.
[1710] She nodded at him.
[1711] "I know what you're thinking. But I have everything that makes life worth living - I may have lost it all - but I once had it. I've suffered a lot physically - but because of the pain, you learn to appreciate the wonderful joy that comes when the pain stops. Moreover, everyone has always been good to me... I am a lucky woman, really."
[1712] Cora Dedalus found an opening in what she had just said.
